No lighting in the parking lot, very little cars and a homeless encampment on the far side of the parking lot (I was in the Sacramento area). I don't think the concept on Ghost Kitchens will last long. There is no way for a brand to ensure quality control if they're outsourcing everything to another restaurant. Ontop of that, the additional cost to outsource the food, and then the doordash fee, and tip, make the food too costly. Which causes them to switch to lower quality and cheaper products to maintain competitive prices. And as we see in reviews, results in a subpar, mediocre burger/food.

No no no no no no no. Ghost kitchens are awful. If they were going into small mom and pop's restaurants who are on the verge of closing and this is a way for them to supplement their income and keep their workers employed, that's one thing. There is no reason for a large national chain like Chili's to run ghost kitchens though. Full clarity, Chili's doesn't carry Mr. Beast Burger, but they do cook like 4 virtual brands on top of their own food, and it is too much. Kitchens are super backed up, and front of house staff is suffering. For a kitchen to be too busy to seat the restaurant and have to run to go only several shifts out of the week is detrimental to the majority of its staff who rely on people coming into the restaurant. I had to move on from my job of 12 years because of this, and I had it better than most. It's a vicious cycle, and serving is a dying profession because of it.

That is hardly the first free restaurant. I volunteer at one in my city, and we have been operational for over 25 years; we give food away to our clients every day.  We get people in the community to give us money and food, then volunteers and staff cook it up and it goes out the front door; we used to let the clients in and let them eat inside, but that changed with the first COVID-19 lock-down.  We do not have a drive-through as most of our clients can not afford a car, but thankfully there is a bus stop only a few hundred metres away.  We also have a food bank where people can get shelf-stable stuff to take home.

As someone who's worked in kitchens, the issue I see with this is that multiple menus means lower quality. The more unrelated dishes your cooks need to prepare, the worse each dish is going to be. This is why incredibly expensive restaurants normally have fairly restrictive menus by comparison to fast food places. When there's a coherent and relatively small menu, the staff is able to make each dish at a level of quality the customer wouldn't be able to reproduce at home.   If multiple unrelated restaurants are operating out of one kitchen, but the number of workers doesn't increase to match that, then every single restaurant's food is going to take a hit in terms of the quality. This also creates a risk of overwork or understaffing, which can lead to things like employee burnout, workplace injuries, or even things like food poisoning.

Food not arriving, people leaving my food on the floor outside instead of giving it to me, food arriving cold, wrong food, its terrible. This only works when the restaurants contracted to make the food can meet the standards for the "brand" and its customers. I think this is a brilliant idea to keep restaurants busy during this time, until restrictions are loosened and they can get back to their normal fare and operation. However I'm already seeing some scattered reviews on both sides of the spectrum - "...inedible fries...soggy, cold buns...missing items...communication poor" as well as "...worth the price...tasty...burgers and fries were great...". So clearly, for this (I assume) temporary brand to be profitable for the participating businesses, there are improvements to be made - namely in consistency of preparation (and therefore flavor) and streamlining the ordering experience.

Virtual kitchens, ie non public facing kitchens for delivery only, have been around for nearly a decade especially in large cities where the restaurant you want to order from is so far away that delivering food in a decent timeframe is almost impossible. These virtual kitchens are held to the same if not greater standards as they must meet requirements for all the brands they cook for.

I'd love to talk about this for a moment, I work in one of those kitchens that uses ghost kitchens. I work the carry out position, and I see what the kitchen staff goes through. I've seen Tyga Bites, Mariahs Cookies, MrBeast Burgers all go out at the same time. Along with the lunch rush. What unfortunately ends up happening is that the original business itself gets super busy too, with carry out orders and dine in customers. While there are dead dine in days we still do get busy, for the past two nights we've been packed in dine in swamped with carry out, and swamped with these third party orders.  I work at Bertucci's and I want to elaborate a bit more on what happens when we get virtual  kitchen orders. it can happen two different ways, it can either print out automatically like normal online orders do, or we have to manually put in the orders, and for MrBeast orders, we have to manually put in the orders every single time. Here's the caveat not all kitchens are built equally. Here's what I mean by that. Not all kitchens were granted with the same space. some are smaller and some are bigger. we don't get a choice when it comes to the virtual kitchens, all the decisions are made by the higher ups, and im not talking bout like managers, or general managers, I'm talking like the general managers boss's boss. We have to put in fryers for the MrBeast burgers, each one needs its own space and it's own equipment. Not all kitchens have the room for that and not all kitchens are able to put in expansions to the building. There's so much I want to say and I want to get across, but there's just too much. I guess all I can say is that yes, virtual kitchens are keeping my Bertucci's location afloat, but at what cost to the employees.
Everything he does legit keeps helping people, by hiring ghost kitchens, he's even helping the restaurants that aren't doing well because of the pandemic-

I was a little annoyed after ordering it. My friends and I drove to Pittsburgh and planned on ordering it to the mall basically right next to the buca that we were ordering from. But they literally wouldn't deliver there. So we eventually got annoyed and was wondering if they were delivering anywhere. We ended up delivering it to the buca itself. Now despite being the "first free restaurant", the food was pretty expensive. I think it was about 70 dollars for the 5 of us. Cans of coke (yes, cans) were about 2 bucks. So we ordered it, and it told us it'd be about 35 minutes. Yes, 35 minutes for someone to drive to the buca and then just give us the food. So we walked over to the mall and then walked back around the time it was time. We noticed our driver come in and then he came back with the food and sat in there with the food for about 5-10 minutes. I sat there texting him we were in the parking lot and what our car was and he eventually drove next to our car (literally like 10 feet) and gave us the food. Now the food quality, it was good, I'll give them that. They seasoned the fries pretty well, though sometimes you'd eat them a little too fast and the spice would burn your nose (yeah it was a little rough). The burger was actually pretty good, it kind of reminded me of five guys but without the free, endless toppings you can get from five guys. In the end, it was kind of like yeah it's cool we did that, but it costed a bit much for fast food and it was a bit of a hassle to get, so we probably wouldn't go back. And no, we did not get any free money, ipads, or airpods

I actually tried ordering one here in Columbus while watching this video and, while the food prices themselves are quire reasonable, the price is a bit absurd. For the food, $11 for fries, a burger, and soda isn't too bad, even with $1.80 in taxes added on to that (because Ohio taxes everything, exuberantly). But then its 4.99 for delivery and $2 for a 'service fee', which I initially thought was a gratuity but no - after you place the order it asks you how much you would like to tip. So, $11 for the food, $1.80 in taxes, $7 in fees, and the deliverer's tip (since I have a soul), came to a total of about $22.  I wish I could tell you if it was worth it, but I just backed out and uninstalled the app at that point, because that is too much for me.

The dark side of this is that restaurants will also make "ghost listings" on services like uber eats and postmates, which takes their original menu and splits it up, just to saturate the listings with more of their own. Hooters is one example, they now have hooters, hooties burger bar, and hooties bait and tackle, which mostly has the same items as the base hooter's, but now takes up 3 spots in your list, pushing out other restaurants with menus just as big, but with only one listing. There are other local restaurants near where I live around charlotte, NC that do this too. They have "cool" names like "mac to the future" which is a back to the future themed mac n cheese place, but it's just mac and cheese from a restaurant that already existed and sold mac and cheese, and the only "themed" part is the picture design. Their food is just regular food. I don't agree with this practice, I think it's just hogging the restaurant list and unfairly pushing out other restaurants.   It's the same method that ghost kitchens use to get in via the various delivery services, but in this case it's malicious. I don't have a suggestion on how to fix it, but I think it does need to be addressed.

In terms of the economics of ghost kitchens; I do agree that they have been extremely successful and have filled a niche of the current market-- however as an industry trends professional of over a decade disagree about the long-term potential. They will succeed as long as restaurant groups such as the one's depicted are operating without indoor dining and with a skeleton staff, simply because they need some avenue of income to cover operating costs. However, once the pandemic has finally been contained, consumer demand will 100% shift back to in-store dining for factors of nostalgia and social interaction. Now, the one thing you briefly mentioned are TRUE remote ghost kitchens, i.e brick and mortar specifically designed as takeout food hubs. These I believe will outlast their co-op counterparts (who will in turn lose this vertical of income), and will change the delivery market long term.

This theory just makes me think of how many small local businesses this type of business hurts.  The small mom & pop burger shop like the one a few miles from me has exactly one location.  They were already on the back foot competing with the big mega chains.  But do you know who donated more money & time to local causes like parks and recreational facilities for kids?  Those mom & pop shops by a wide margin.  Now perhaps thatâ€™s anecdotal, your local mom & pop shop could be run by a pair of old misers who take every dime they earn from you and spend it in Cabo.  But statically small mom & pop shops have a higher rates of income being redistributed locally.  Itâ€™s been a while since I looked this stuff up, but a few years ago it was something like for every dollar you spend at local business 80-90% of of which stayed local in the form of wages for employees, and other production costs.  With big Mega Chains that number drops to something closer to 20%.  This new virtual location scheme seems intended to drop that value of money spent staying in the local area as close to zero as possible.  I donâ€™t have the numbers here to compare, but Iâ€™d wager for every Maria Cookie youâ€™re buying, the grinchâ€™s share of that cost is getting shipped out of state.  But itâ€™s not like we live in a society where the vast majority of money is increasingly controlled by a smaller and smaller minority of people while the average person is fighting harder and harder for a smaller and smaller piece of whatâ€™s left...

They don't need to be at a prominent location, they could be anywhere. The restaurant locations usually require such high rents but you wouldn't need to pay that much with virtual restaurants. Furthermore, you could open more restaurants to shorten the delivery time instead of tying up a massive area to one restaurant.

The basic concept of this isn't actually new. It's just applied to something that is completely different. Allow me to explain. "Ghostwriting" is when an author pays someone else to write their book for them, then the author publishes it under their name and (hopefully) the ghostwriter (not rider, writer, there's a difference) gets the cut of the profit alongside the author as does the publisher of the book. This practice has been a thing for many years. The concept (and the practice although not for writing books in this case) has been injected into the fast food industry thanks to the help of technology and it works for the hard times we're living in. A surprise to be sure, but a welcomed one, as it will help keep businesses afloat.

Ghost kitchens are good options for existing restaurants lacking customers. But there's a darker side to that coin.   A lot of ghost kitchens are sadly not up to code with too much staff and equipment crammed into a small space without even ventilation. Causing major health risks to the people working at those places.  And that is ignoring the long hours which are typical in that field of work (workweeks of 70+h isn't too uncommon). Yes delivery brands might set up ghost kitchens themselves. But that can also just be a container on some outskirts of town.

This is not a new idea, only for the food industry. Ever heard of Interflora or FastFlowers? Same thing. Except, florists hate them. We call then order gatherers because all they do is take an order and make someone else make it up. The truth is the business making it up gets very very little money. Think of the cute everyone is getting from an order where the customer is not paying a price higher than average. Order gatherers also do some seriously dodgy practices to get those orders over actual florists. I recommend googling the problem of order gatherers and having a read. I'm sure the food industry will easily see the same problems.

Then again... I have eaten from the other kind of ghost restaurant. One that actually never existed. Back in college, several of us were pitching together on a house off campus. One day, there was this door hanger for "Roller Chicken" - Delivery fried chicken. Logo was a chicken on roller skates.  Cool! we thought. We scrabbled together our loose bills and change, counted up enough for an order and tip, and called it in. Food was delivered, it was delicious! and we were a happy pack of college students. Few days later. We decided to get another order. We called the number. The exact words have been forgotten (this was over 30 years ago) but essentially went like: "We'd like to place an order." True "ghost kitchens" aren't actual restaurants at all; they exist solely as kitchens for virtual restaurant and food brands. MrBeastBurger uses more of a franchise model, working with existing restaurants (but specifically not ghost kitchens) who prepare his products alongside their own. It isn't a HUGE distinction, but it's important to understand that MrBeastBurger is explicitly working with actual restaurants, providing additional streams of revenue for an industry suffering under the impacts of COVID. He is specifically not contracted with any virtual-only kitchens.

Mat pat has big sacs for the macs
This ghost kitchen concept I feel as an equal chance to go big as it is to go broke in the long term. If you think about it, the pandemic DID enable the rise of food delivery and delivery apps. The problem lies is what happens AFTER the pandemic. Ideally when we get to a point ~70% of the total population is vaccinated and herd immunity kicks in to halt covid-19 (and yes even if there are mutations it could be a yearly vaccine like the flu) then what? People aren't just going to go out of eat anymore? Here in the States eating is as much a social activity than anything else, and many people are lazy and don't want to cook (in my part of the country landscapers and caterers are big business because people have money but don't want to actually cook/do yard-work) and eventually there will come a point where restaurants will open again. What then? The niche ghost kitchens filled will vanish, and those kitchens may start to drop the celebrity brands, or at the very least push them into a smaller corner. Sure people will still want to order delivery, but when post-pandemic hits the demand for it probably won't be nearly that high.  Also you forgot the app delivery drivers. We all know by now those companies classify their drivers as "contractors" on paper. That gives the the ability to deny them benefits and dodge liability in case of injuries on the job, in addition to preventing unionizing. Everyone took it from the original "business contractors playbook" Vince McMahon (it could have been senior though I'm not entirely sure). WWE wrestlers have been the original "contractors", McMahon using that loophole to ensure his company isn't liable for wrestlers injuries, healthcare costs, and to ensure they continue to work (often times to breaking) without proper compensation. If this ghost kitchen does take off and be the "new face of dining" then what happens to the drivers?  Prop 22 in California was a harsh blow to this issue, big companies pouring millions into marketing to convince the public it was "good for business". What it did was ensure delivery drivers for grubhub, door dash, and the like can be classified as "independent contractors". Which allows the companies to avoid providing government-mandates services like minimum wages, health benefits, and even bargaining powers which employees have.  The entire issue is much larger than a simple comment here, but the fact remains. Whether ghost kitchens as a concepts goes boom or goes bust in the next several years, there will be more issues at play than simply changing the restaurant/dining industry game.

We talked about this over at Swell Entertainment's comment section, but it's worth putting here too:  The model isn't new. It's been around for almost as long as kitchens have been around. The main issues that plague Ghost Kitchen concepts are based around efficiency. Fast Food restaurants, like McDonalds, optimize their production line to handle the products they sell. It only takes a few minutes to make their food because their machines and kitchen design are specifically designed to handle the volume and type of food they make. Their kitchen matches their menu.  In a Ghost Kitchen environment, the kitchen has to be designed to allow for almost any type of food. The kitchen staff have to all be trained in an extremely wide array of menu items, and the expo people have to be really on point with setting up orders to be taken. In the past, the Ghost Kitchen suffered because of the amount of time it takes for a normal kitchen to produce the same food as a specialized fast food kitchen.  On top of this, fast food restaurants are highly standardized so that the quality of the food is the same across the board. There should be next to zero differences between locations. With Ghost Kitchens, because of staffing rotations, one cook might salt heavier than another cook. A cook might personally prefer their hamburger meat to not be fully cooked and thus send out undercooked hamburgers. Another, like myself, might know that the requirement for ground meat in food service is that it be fully cooked (Well done in common speech) which will then have people receive a different quality of burger from the same location.  All said, the Ghost Kitchen model works for fast casual chains like Buca et al. because they're pseudo-fast food anyways. There will always be a market for it, but there will also always be issues with it in the long run. I support them, as a chef still in the industry, but I caution consumers to be aware that wait times can drastically vary between locations and brands.

They'll be quickly having from 30 to 100 orders to fill at a certain time from one day to another, and it will become a complete mess.  I do hope that people adapt themselves so that this "safe" model is viable, but I don't think so.

Ghost kitchens will certainly be one of the major factors to increase the profitability of food delivery apps. Someone can set up a commissary kitchen that doesn't have any dine-in options and only cater to food delivery or food truck prep. And several restaurants, even competitors, can get their food made in a commissary kitchen and those companies don't have to pay people to cook because the commissary kitchen and hire their own employees and train them cross-platform to cook whatever is on the menu. And because they don't necessarily have to worry about everything being made to order they could have things close to being already made that way it doesn't take that much time to finish preparation.
